            ÂæûÊ≠§Ê¨°ÊúÉË≠∞‰∏≠‰∏âÊ°àÁöÑÊ±∫Ë≠∞ÂèØ‰ª•ÁôºÁèæÔºåÊ≠§ÊôÇ‰ª£Âè∞ÁÅ£ÊîøÂ∫úÂú®ÊîøÁ≠ñÂ∞éÂêë‰∏äÊòéÁ¢∫ÂÆ£Á§∫ÔºåÂÆóÊïôÊîøÁ≠ñÂÇæÂêëÊñºÂà∂ÂÆöÂÆóÊïôÊ≥ï‰æÜÁ´ãÊ≥ïË¶èÁØÑÂÆóÊïô‰∫ãÂãô„ÄÇËÄåÂú®Ê≠§Âæå1979Âπ¥Ë°åÊîøÈô¢ÈÄöÈÅéÂÖßÊîøÈÉ®Êì¨Ë®ÇÁöÑ„ÄåÂØ∫ÂªüÊïôÂ†ÇÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄçËçâÊ°à„ÄÇ

            Deliberation on Religious Legislation

            After relocating to Taiwan in 1949, the Republic of China (ROC) government continued to regulate temples led by Buddhist monks, Taoist priests, and other religious clergy based on the Regulations on the Supervision of Temples, which had been promulgated in China in 1929. However, due to changing circumstances, this regulation was no longer sufficient to govern the religious landscape in Taiwan. To establish a framework for religious legislation, the Ministry of the Interior (MOI) began collecting relevant domestic and international laws in 1953 and subsequently drafted several legislative proposals.

            A review of government regulations and legal cases related to religion during this period reveals some of the earliest official attempts at religious legislation. On August 26, 1955, the MOI convened a Conference on the Deliberation and Formulation of Religious Laws, inviting representatives from the Kuomintang Central Committee, Taiwan Provincial Government, Taiwan Provincial Security Command, and Taiwan Provincial Police Department. This meeting resulted in recorded discussions about drafting religious laws. (Wu Huichiao, 2005, p. 189)

            The conference addressed three key proposals:

            1. The necessity of enacting a religious law or church law for the supervision, management, and protection of religion.

              • The resolution affirmed the necessity of enacting such laws based on practical needs.

                1. Principles for drafting a religious law.

                  • The resolution outlined four key principles:

                    1. The law must not violate the constitutional principle of religious freedom and should establish a clear national religious policy.

                      1. It must not contradict Taiwan‚Äôs diplomatic treaty obligations and should promote domestic religious development.

                        1. It should adopt a proactive guidance approach rather than a passive laissez-faire attitude.

                          1. Protection should take precedence over control, and guidance should be prioritized over supervision.

                            1. Procedures for drafting the religious law.

                              • The resolution assigned the MOI to draft the religious law and distribute it to relevant agencies for review and feedback. After collecting responses, the MOI would convene a meeting with reputable figures from various religious groups to further discuss the proposal. (Wu Huichiao, 2005, p. 189)

                                The resolutions from this conference indicate that the Taiwanese government at the time had a clear policy orientation toward legislating religious affairs. Following this initial effort, in 1979, the Executive Yuan approved the Draft Regulations on Temples and Churches, which had been formulated by the MOI.

                                ‰∏âÊòØÊµ∑Â§ñÁöÑÂã¢ÂäõÔºåÂõ†ÁÇ∫Âú®Ê≠§ÊïèÊÑüÂíåÁà≠Ë≠∞ÁöÑÊôÇÂàªÔºåÁéãÊ∞∏‰ø°Âú®1964Âπ¥Âá∫Áâà‰∫Ü„ÄäÁúüÈÅìÊâãÂÜä„ÄãÔºåÂÖ∂‰∏≠ÈáùÂ∞çÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÂçîÁöÑÊâπÂà§ÊñáÂ≠óÔºåÂ∞áÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÂçîÊåáÁÇ∫ÊïµÂü∫Áù£ÔºåÈÄôÊú¨Êõ∏Âæå‰æÜÊàê‰∫ÜÊâìÊìäÁï∞Á´ØÁöÑÊåáÂçó„ÄÇÂÖ®ÊñáËΩâËºâÊñº1966Âπ¥4Êúà24Êó•ÊâÄÂá∫„ÄäÁ¶èÈü≥Â†±„ÄãÁ¨¨296ÊúüÔºåÁõ¥ÊåáÊôÆ‰∏ñÊïôÂçîÁöÑÂêà‰∏ÄÈÅãÂãïÊòØÁèæ‰ª£ÊúÄÂ§ßÁöÑ„ÄåÁï∞Á´Ø„ÄçÔºå„ÄäÁúüÈÅìÊâãÂÜä„ÄãÂπæ‰πé‰∫∫Êâã‰∏ÄÂÜäÔºåÊàê‰∫ÜÂèçÂ∞çÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁöÑÊÄùÊÉ≥ÊåáÂ∞éÁ∂±È†ò„ÄÇÔºàÂªñÊñåÊ¥≤Ôºå2017Ôºå62-63Ôºâ

                                Three Forces Within Christianity Opposing the Centennial Assembly

                                Three major factions within Christianity opposed the World Council of Churches (WCC) and its ecumenical movement in Taiwan:

                                1. American Fundamentalist Organizations Opposing the WCC

                                  • The first faction consisted of American fundamentalist groups affiliated with the International Council of Christian Churches (ICCC), which had long opposed the WCC. Key figures included Dr. Cheng Wen-Hsiu, founder of the Evangelical Friends Church, and McIntire, president of the ICCC. Their primary propaganda outlet was The Trumpet Weekly, which condemned the WCC‚Äôs ecumenical movement as an attempt to build the Tower of Babel in the Last Days. (Liao Binzhou, 2017, pp. 62-63)

                                    • This faction actively obstructed the participation of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) in the WCC, which had begun as early as 1951. Their strategy involved Cheng Wen-Hsiu‚Äôs establishment of various anti-communist Christian organizations, including the ICCC China Chapter (1961), the Asian Christian Apologetics Anti-Communist Association (1965), and the World Christian Apologetics Anti-Communist Association (1969).

                                      1. Domestic Christian Organizations Promoting Apologetics and Anti-Communism

                                        • The second faction was composed of domestic Christian groups advocating for apologetics and anti-communism. Key figures included Zhang Jingyu, who founded the Christian Apologetics and Anti-Communism Union in 1962 and served as its first president; Reverend Chen Xizhen of Shuanglian Presbyterian Church; and Kang Junbi, an official in the Ministry of the Interior who also served as General Secretary of the Christian Gospel Fellowship and President of the Christian Salvation Service Association.

                                          • In 1967, Kang became General Secretary of the Taiwan Christian Association and attended the 14th General Assembly of the PCT. During his speech, he openly incited and pressured assembly members to withdraw from the WCC, even threatening Reverend Chou Lien-Hua to participate in anti-communist conferences. With quasi-official backing, this faction exerted significant influence. (Dong Fangyuan, 2011)

                                            • Their primary propaganda platform was The Gospel Newspaper, edited by Huang Yuehan, President of the China Laity Evangelistic Fellowship.

                                              1. Overseas Christian Influence

                                                • The third faction involved overseas forces. Amidst this politically sensitive and controversial period, Wang Yongxin published The Handbook of Truth in 1964, which harshly criticized the WCC, labeling it as an Antichrist organization. This book became a key reference for combating heresy and was later fully reprinted in The Gospel Newspaper, Issue 296 (April 24, 1966). It directly denounced the WCC‚Äôs ecumenical movement as the greatest heresy of modern times. The Handbook of Truth was widely circulated, becoming a guiding ideological text for opposing the Presbyterian Church. (Liao Binzhou, 2017, pp. 62-63)

                                                  3.Âú∞Á∑£ÊîøÊ≤ªÁí∞Â¢ÉÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§Èóú‰øÇ

                                                  Anti-Communism, Anti-Soviet Campaigns, and the Call to Retake the Mainland

                                                  From the 1950s to the 1960s, the Anti-Communism and Anti-Soviet campaign was a fundamental national policy and key political propaganda strategy of the Republic of China (ROC) government in Taiwan, Penghu, Kinmen, and Matsu (known as the ‚ÄúFree Area‚Äù). Since the KMT government had retreated to Taiwan following the Chinese Civil War, it simultaneously promoted the policy of Retaking the Mainland. These policies spanned the presidencies of Chiang Kai-shek (five terms), Yen Chia-kan, and Chiang Ching-kuo. The term Communism referred specifically to the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), while Soviet referred to the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics (USSR) and the Communist Party of the Soviet Union (CPSU).

                                                  For Taiwanese people who grew up before the 1970s, Anti-Communism and Anti-Soviet and Retaking the Mainland were frequently heard slogans. The government portrayed the Soviet Union as an invader and the CCP, which received Soviet support, as traitors to the Chinese nation. The rhetoric emphasized the survival of the nation and ethnic integrity. Chiang Kai-shek, who was both President and Chairman of the KMT at the time, declared the slogan:

                                                  ‚ÄúOne year of preparation, two years of counterattack, three years of clearing the enemy, and five years of victory.‚Äù

                                                  These slogans were aggressively promoted to the Taiwanese public, deeply embedding the idea into society. (Yeh Huifen, 2016, p. 149)

                                                  Beyond propaganda, Chiang Kai-shek actively planned to use remnants of the ROC Army that had been unable to retreat from mainland China as internal resistance forces. The KMT also used Hong Kong and its controlled territories as intelligence intermediaries to coordinate guerrilla warfare against the CCP. The U.S., under its anti-communist policy, supported Chiang by strengthening Taiwan‚Äôs military capabilities. However, publicly, the U.S. avoided escalating the conflict into a renewed Chinese Civil War that could trigger World War

  1. (Lin Xiaoting, 2015, pp. 104-107)

    Despite U.S. reservations, Chiang Kai-shek secretly formulated military plans. In 1956, the Ministry of National Defense launched Project Triumph at the Shilin Practical Studies Institute. In 1957, it established the Zhongxing Project Office as a mission-oriented command unit, and in 1961, the Guoguang Operations Office was set up in Sanxia, Taipei County. Over the next four decades, numerous military operations were developed, including Project 5504, Project Triumph, Project Zhongxing, and Project Tian Dan. (Yeh Huifen, 2016, pp. 153-155)

    However, later scholars, with access to declassified documents, discovered that during the Korean War in the 1950s, the U.S. actually wanted Chiang Kai-shek to counterattack the mainland, creating a second front to relieve pressure on U.S. forces in Korea. American politicians frequently visited Taiwan, lobbying Chiang and even promising military aid for an invasion. Ironically, it was Chiang Kai-shek who rejected the idea, refusing to launch a counterattack. Scholars argue that Retaking the Mainland was merely a slogan‚ÄîChiang‚Äôs true intention was to consolidate KMT rule in Taiwan. (Lin Xiaoting, 2017, pp. 288-296)

    Moreover, the rhetoric of Anti-Communism was not absolute. The phrase ‚ÄúThere can be no coexistence between Chinese patriots and traitors‚Äù (Êº¢Ë≥ä‰∏çÂÖ©Á´ã), often used to attack the CCP, did not prevent secret exchanges between the two sides. During the height of the 1958 Second Taiwan Strait Crisis (823 Artillery Bombardment), rumors of secret communications between Taiwan and Beijing were widespread. Beijing reportedly used Hong Kong as a backchannel to negotiate with Taiwanese leaders, offering not to invade Kinmen and Matsu in exchange for Chiang reducing reliance on U.S. military aid. This agreement also included the return of the Dachen Islands to Taiwan. When the CIA learned of these exchanges, it was alarmed. Under U.S. pressure, Chiang publicly reaffirmed Taiwan‚Äôs commitment to Anti-Communism and denied any secret dealings with the CCP.

    Similarly, Chiang secretly engaged in diplomacy with the Soviet Union in the 1960s, hoping to gain Soviet support for a counterattack on the mainland. He strategically played both sides‚Äînegotiating with both the CCP and the USSR while leveraging these interactions in his geopolitical maneuvering with the U.S. (Lin Xiaoting, 2017)

    In 1972, the ROC government disbanded the Guoguang Operations Office, signaling the end of Retaking the Mainland as a primary national objective. By 1991, the government officially ended the Mobilization for the Suppression of Communist Rebellion (ÂãïÂì°Êà°‰∫Ç), marking a new era in cross-strait relations.

    Korea and the Korean War

    Among East Asian regions, including Taiwan, Japan, Mongolia, and mainland China, Christianity remains a minority religion. However, in South Korea, Christianity has a significantly higher proportion of adherents compared to other religious groups. (Robert Koehler, 2012)

    Historically, Confucianism was the dominant ideology in the Korean Peninsula, making it one of the last regions to encounter mainstream Christian denominations. The earliest Christian influence came from China, where a large number of Western missionaries arrived in the 16th century, leading some Confucian scholars of the Ming dynasty to convert to Christianity (Catholicism). (Choi, 1984, p. 4) In 1603, a Joseon envoy to the Ming dynasty obtained Christian theological books written by Matteo Ricci from Confucian scholars and brought them back to Korea, marking the first introduction of Christianity to the peninsula. Christianity, particularly Catholicism, faced suppression in 1866 but re-entered Korea in 1884 when missionaries from the United States and other countries reopened the peninsula to Christian influence.

    During Japan‚Äôs colonial rule over Korea, the government implemented forced assimilation policies. However, unlike indigenous Korean traditions, Christianity, with its Western connections, was given some space to operate. This period saw significant Christian movements, such as the Wonsan Revival Movement in 1903 and the Pyongyang Great Revival in 1907. Christianity in Korea became intertwined with nationalist movements, giving it a different meaning compared to other countries and laying the groundwork for its post-war expansion. (Colin Whittaker, 1988, p. 65) By 1924, a nationwide Christian council had been established, and Christianity had begun to gain influence in Korean society.

    Following the end of Japanese occupation, the Korean War (1950‚Äì1953) became a turning point for the Korean Peninsula. The war involved North Korea, supported by the People‚Äôs Republic of China and the Soviet Union, against South Korea, which was backed by a United Nations coalition of 16 countries, led primarily by the United States. The war began on June 25, 1950, when North Korean forces crossed the 38th parallel and invaded the South. The conflict ended with the signing of the Korean Armistice Agreement at Panmunjom on July 27, 1953, establishing the Korean Demilitarized Zone (DMZ) along the ceasefire line as a buffer zone.

    The Korean War also had a profound impact on China‚Äôs geopolitical landscape. China‚Äôs intervention prompted the United States to deploy the Seventh Fleet to the Taiwan Strait, leading to a shift in U.S. policy toward Taiwan and solidifying the cross-strait confrontation that persists to this day. (Bruce Elleman, 2017, pp. 38, 70)

    Before the Korean War, two-thirds of Korea‚Äôs Christians resided in the northern region. However, as the war escalated, North Korea, influenced by Soviet-style atheism, adopted state atheism as a key component of its Juche (self-reliance) ideology, prompting most Christians to flee southward to seek religious freedom in South Korea. In the years between the end of the Korean War and South Korea‚Äôs democratization, Christianity experienced a conversion boom.

    During the 1960s, churches extended aid to marginalized groups, including sex workers and industrial laborers. As South Korea underwent rapid economic development, labor issues became a central concern of Christian missionary work, leading to the establishment of industrial chaplaincy programs for factory workers. (Gelder, Craig Van, 2007, p.

  1. By the 1970s, South Korean Christians had increasingly engaged in social justice movements, contributing to the development of minjung theology (ÎØºÏ§ëÏã†Ìïô, the people‚Äôs theology), which interpreted the gospel through the lens of Korea‚Äôs political and social realities. (Suh, 1983, p. 17)
